  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_8958bbf1-7dfd-4715-9999-28c07b7dc46e'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

<span id="freeTextContainer10094702173557675629">كُتب الكثير عن بطولات ومآثر الحروب، وعن مدى الحاجة إليها بوصفها وسيلةً لتحقيق أهداف قد تُعدُّ نبيلة. لكن بقي السؤال الدائم: هل يوجد تبرير للسلام ولسعادتنا وحتى للانسجام الأبدي، إذا ما ذُرفت دمعةٌ صغيرةٌ واحدة لطفلٍ بريءٍ في سبيل ذلك؟<br/>في الحرب العالمية الثانية، قُتل وجُرح وهُجِّر أكثر من مئة مليون شخص في حرب هي الأكثر دموية –حتى الآن – في تاريخنا البشري. وقد كُتب الكثير عن م</span>
  <span id="freeText10094702173557675629" style="display:none">كُتب الكثير عن بطولات ومآثر الحروب، وعن مدى الحاجة إليها بوصفها وسيلةً لتحقيق أهداف قد تُعدُّ نبيلة. لكن بقي السؤال الدائم: هل يوجد تبرير للسلام ولسعادتنا وحتى للانسجام الأبدي، إذا ما ذُرفت دمعةٌ صغيرةٌ واحدة لطفلٍ بريءٍ في سبيل ذلك؟<br />في الحرب العالمية الثانية، قُتل وجُرح وهُجِّر أكثر من مئة مليون شخص في حرب هي الأكثر دموية –حتى الآن – في تاريخنا البشري. وقد كُتب الكثير عن مآسي ونتائج هذه المرحلة القاتمة من تاريخنا. ولكن كيف رآها آخر الشهود الأحياء؛ أطفال هذه الحرب؟<br />بعد أكثر من ثلاثين عاماً على نهاية تلك الحرب تُعيد سفيتلانا في كتابها آخر الشهود مَن بقي من أبطال تلك المرحلة إلى طفولتهم التي عايشت الحرب، لتروي على لسانهم آخر الكلمات... عن زمان يُختتم بهم...</span>

<div id="review_320055900" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/320055900" />
    <a title="Nataliya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya"><img alt="Nataliya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605995014p2/3672777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/320055900?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nataliya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nataliya" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya">Nataliya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/42963288-last-witnesses">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=war-s-inhuman-face">war-s-inhuman-face</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=russian-classics-reviewed">russian-classics-reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2012-reads">2012-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer320055900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15976447044753803109"><br/><b>
  <i>"I am a person without childhood. Instead of childhood, I had war."</i>
</b><br/><br/>This is undeniably the most horrifying book I've ever read. It's the first book that actually made my heart <i>physically ache</i>. <b>Reading it was like taking a razor blade to my soul.</b> It was like slowly tearing my heart into bleeding pieces.<blockquote>
  <i>How can we preserve our planet on which little girls are supposed to sleep in their beds and not lie dead on the road with unplaited pigtails? So that childhood would never again be called wa</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText15976447044753803109" style="display:none"><br /><b>
  <i>"I am a person without childhood. Instead of childhood, I had war."</i>
</b><br /><br />This is undeniably the most horrifying book I've ever read. It's the first book that actually made my heart <i>physically ache</i>. <b>Reading it was like taking a razor blade to my soul.</b> It was like slowly tearing my heart into bleeding pieces.<blockquote>
  <i>How can we preserve our planet on which little girls are supposed to sleep in their beds and not lie dead on the road with unplaited pigtails? So that childhood would never again be called war-time childhood.</i>
</blockquote> Collected by Svetlana Alexievich, the author of the similarly soul-shattering <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/357486.Voices_from_Chernobyl_The_Oral_History_of_a_Nuclear_Disaster" title="Voices from Chernobyl The Oral History of a Nuclear Disaster by Светлана Алексиевич" rel="nofollow noopener">Voices from Chernobyl: The Oral History of a Nuclear Disaster</a>, these are the stories told by former "war children" who have survived the Great Patriotic War - Nazis against the Soviets - which lasted four years and took 27 million of Soviet lives. The Nazi had no qualms ruthlessly murdering Slavs and Jews, regarding them as subhuman, even less than animals, deserving to die for the 'crime' of simply existing. <b>Simple and unbelievably horrifying, the stories of the childhood shattered by war made me cry and gasp in horror at the atrocities seen by children who had their innocence brutally ripped away and saw the absolute worst that can ever happen to anyone.</b> <blockquote>
  <i>"The war did not end quickly. They count - four years. Four years of shooting... But how many years of trying to forget?"</i>
</blockquote>I've heard similar stories before - <b>my grandparents lived though a "war childhood" as well.</b> Grandpa was seven when the war began, living in occupied Belarus. He still finds it hard to talk about constant hunger, daily threat of death, burned-down villages, murdered neighbors... Grandma was six, living in a relative safety of Kazakhstan, where the main enemy was hunger and soup made from grass was dinner. When she asked for 'real' food, her mother would tell her through tears to go to sleep instead, because then you can at least dream of food. <b>To this day, my grandparents have never thrown out a single piece of bread. To this day, they detest any kind of war, no matter how righteous it may seem.</b><blockquote>
  <i>"The Germans were going from house to house. They gathered the mothers of those who went to join the partisans. And they cut their heads off in the middle of the village. We were ordered, "Watch"... I want to forget it all..."</i>
</blockquote>The stories of the former "war children" in this book are all different - some were in the occupied territories, some were on the front lines, some were in the underground guerilla resistance - the partisans, some were in Leningrad under 900-day siege, others were evacuated to safer parts of the country, yet others were forcibly sent to Germany as slaves. But the common theme is the extreme pain of separation from their parents and facing death daily - their own and that of their loved ones. <b>They all were subjected to things that no child should witness. And their perception of the horrors around them was though that peculiar simple childlike honest gaze that leaves the reader speechless and shaken.</b><br /><br />The villages were occupied by the Germans, with people often forced out of their homes into "zemlyanki", makeshift 'homes' that were basically holes in the ground, even in winter months. There was terrible hunger, and stealing a bit of wheat that now belonged to the occupants was immediate death, even if the 'thief' was a toddler. <b>Age did not matter - the supposedly 'subhuman' Soviet children got the same treatment as the adults. Usually it was death.</b><blockquote>
  <i>"Many years later I learned that they gauged out mom's eyes and tore out her hair, cut off her breasts. They sicced dogs on little sister Galya who hid under the tree and refused to come out. The dogs brought her out - piece by piece. Mom was still alive, she understood everything... Right in front of her..."</i>
</blockquote>Most villages had people - usually young men and women - join the partisans, the guerilla resistance in the woods. The punishment for knowing the partisans, helping them, being related to them was death - often for the entire village, children included. The 'guilty' were publicly executed - shot, beaten to death, hanged - and everyone, including their children, had to watch. <b>They were not allowed to cry - the punishment was, again, death. Even for children.</b><blockquote>
  <i>"They were shooting three people at a time. They would put them by the edge of the hole - and shoot at point blank range. The rest were watching... I don't remember parents saying goodbye to their children or children to their parents. One mother lifted the hem of her dress and covered her daughter's eyes. But even the little children did not cry..."</i>
</blockquote>The soldiers were methodically going from home to home, gunning down women, children, and the elderly. Sometimes they would burn them alive. Sometimes they'd force all the people into the open, interrogate them, and shoot either randomly, or shoot everyone. Those who survived, did so by unbelievable stroke of luck. That is, if you can call it 'luck' - watching all your loved ones, your friends, your neighbors brutally murdered.<blockquote>
  <i>"The whole street burned. Grandmas and grandpas burned, and many small children because they did not run away with everybody else, they thought the Germans would not touch them. The fire did not spare anyone. You're walking - and see a black corpse, that means the old person has burned. And if you see from far away something small and pink - it means a child. They were lying on the coals, pink..."</i>
</blockquote>In the cities, things were not any better. The Jews were forced into the ghettos. The crematoriums in the concentration camps were welcoming Jews and Slavs alike. And the small children - they were taken to forcibly give blood since <i>"German doctors believed that blood of children under age five helped quick recovery of the wounded. Had a youthful effect. I know that now..."</i> <b>The children, drained of most of their blood, either quickly died or got sick and were thrown into the concentration camps crematoriums. </b><blockquote>
  <i>We used to eat... water. At dinner time, mom would put a pot of hot water on the table. And we would pour it into our bowls. Evening. Supper. There is a pot of hot water on the table.</i>
</blockquote>The children in Leningrad during the 900-day siege were dying of hunger. They ate grass, wallpaper, dirt. They ate their pets - and there were no pets left in the city. <b>They watched everyone die. My heart breaks.</b><blockquote>
  <i>"In this orphanage where I was, they had only children from Leningrad. They could not feed us enough for a long time. We would sit in class and chew paper. They fed us carefully... I was sitting at a table at breakfast. And I saw a cat. I jumped up, "Cat! Cat!" All the kids saw it and started chasing it, "Cat! Cat!" The teachers were local, they looked at us as though we were crazy. In Leningrad, there had been no cats left... Finding a live cat was a dream. Food for the whole month..."</i>
</blockquote>Thousands of children lost their parents, some forever. They were raised in orphanages, learning to go through life in as much of a "normal" way as possible, but longing to have something to call their own, longing for their parents, starved for parental love so much they would approach strangers and ask to be held - like mom would do.<blockquote>
  <i>"At night, there was wailing. We were calling for mom and dad. Our teachers tried to avoid saying the word 'mom' around us. They told us fairy-tales and found books without this word. If anyone ever said "mom", the crying would start immediately. Inconsolable crying."</i>
</blockquote>But despite the horrors, the murders, the pain, the losses there was a common thread running though everyone's stories - <b>the unbelievable kindness and compassion.</b> People did not hesitate to take in the lost and orphaned children who were complete strangers to them even if it meant dividing already miserly food portions into even smaller pieces. They took the children in even if it could mean sure death for harboring a Jewish or a partisan's kid. They gave them everything they had, every last bit, to the children who were complete strangers to them. They sacrificed their lives to save the children who were strangers. <b>There did not seem to be any strangers in the war - everyone was united by the common horror that they all shared.</b><blockquote>
  <i>"What do I have left from the war? I don't understand what strangers are, because my brother and I grew up among strangers. We were saved by strangers. But how can they be strangers to me?"</i>
</blockquote>What really shocked me, however, was the kindness and compassion that children and adults alike were showing to their former enemies - the German POWs. Some children were astonished as their mothers gave them the ultimate "lessons in kindness" by sharing their tiny food rations with those who have treated them as subhuman, who killed their loved ones. The Leningrad children, recovering from 900 days of murder and starvation, were sharing their food with those who tried to starve them to death a few years prior. <b>They were so heartbreakingly HUMAN in this because they knew the horror and the price of suffering, and found incredible strength to forgive.</b> How could their hearts be so generous? I will never fully understand, and for that I am incredibly lucky.<br /><br />It's been decades since that war, but these stories still need to be heard. They cannot be forgotten. The absolute horror that war was should always be remembered. It should always remind us of the fragility of human lives, of the shattered lives and destroyed childhoods. The memory, the pain, the warning. <b>Nothing, I repeat - nothing can EVER justify a war like this. Nothing can justify the stolen lives of the innocent. Nothing can justify the scars that will remain forever. Nothing.</b><blockquote>
  <i>"But I can never be really happy. Completely happy. I can't do happiness. I'm afraid of happiness. I always think that it's about to end. This "about to end" always lives inside me. Childhood fear..."</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="15976447044753803109" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2875651261" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16124817288240243239">Originally released in Russia in the eighties, the translated copy is soon to be released here for what I believe is the first time. If you've read others by this author you know she gathers up first hand accounts of various events and catastrophies, then arranged them with little or no change. This book is a heartbreaker as most are when children are concerned. She interviews the now grown people, eliciting from them there memories of war, the Nazi invasion of Russia. There are a range of ages,</span>
  <span id="freeText16124817288240243239" style="display:none">Originally released in Russia in the eighties, the translated copy is soon to be released here for what I believe is the first time. If you've read others by this author you know she gathers up first hand accounts of various events and catastrophies, then arranged them with little or no change. This book is a heartbreaker as most are when children are concerned. She interviews the now grown people, eliciting from them there memories of war, the Nazi invasion of Russia. There are a range of ages, the youngest was four. She gives their names, what their present occupation is and what age they were in their first remembrances.<br /><br />"Then all the colors disappeared. All shades, for the first time the word death appeared; everybody began to repeat this incomprehensible word. And mama and papa weren't there."<br /><br />"I'm a man without a childhood. Instead of a childhood, I have the war."<br /> <br />"During the war I hadn't seen a single child's thing. I forgot they existed. Children's toys......"<br /><br />The author beArs witness, she gives those who experienced the unimaginable a place to tell and share their stories, their memories. I applaud her for this. So this isn't an easy read, but along with the horror was kindness, someone who cared when no one else was left that belonged to them.<br /><br />ARC from Netgalley.<br /><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_2875330198" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2875330198" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2875330198?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141983554" href="/book/show/43157098-last-witnesses">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=russia">russia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=ww2">ww2</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=uzbekistan">uzbekistan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=short">short</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=kids">kids</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=philo-psychol">philo-psychol</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=bio">bio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible-uk">audible-uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2019-read">2019-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2875330198" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12124158979520631832">“<b>Stunning stories about what it was like to be a Soviet child during the upheaval and horror of the Second World War, from Nobel Laureate Svetlana Alexievich</b>”. <br/>(From the GR book description.)<br/><br/><b>
  <u>EVERYBODY</u>
</b> should read or listen to this book. Perhaps those who do not want to read it, need to read it most. <br/><br/>The personal is revealed.<br/>It is devoid of analysis.<br/>It is devoid of blab.<br/>It looks at the emotional and long-lasting consequences of war on a personal level. <br/><br/>Here is the nitty-gritty of war.<br/><br/>The book</span>
  <span id="freeText12124158979520631832" style="display:none">“<b>Stunning stories about what it was like to be a Soviet child during the upheaval and horror of the Second World War, from Nobel Laureate Svetlana Alexievich</b>”. <br />(From the GR book description.)<br /><br /><b>
  <u>EVERYBODY</u>
</b> should read or listen to this book. Perhaps those who do not want to read it, need to read it most. <br /><br />The personal is revealed.<br />It is devoid of analysis.<br />It is devoid of blab.<br />It looks at the emotional and long-lasting consequences of war on a personal level. <br /><br />Here is the nitty-gritty of war.<br /><br />The book will appeal to those who appreciate the straightforward over the intellectual, esoteric, obscure and abstruse. It will appeal to those who appreciate bare, simple prose and honesty.<br /><br />Each "story" is short, concise, to the point.<br /><br />Heartrending but without doubt definitely worth reading. One cannot read this book without tears welling up. Kindness and compassion for others is shown too--for example, children in an orphanage <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[without siblings, parents or any relative pair up with another and look out for each other <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. No, I do not want to give you examples, the book is <b>filled</b> with examples. Complete strangers help other complete strangers, over and over and over again. The inclusion of the good and not just deplorable human behavior makes the book readable. It gives balance.<br /> <br />Julia Emelin, Yelena Shmulenson and Allen Lewis Rickman narrate the audiobook. It is extremely well read, by all three. I do favor the women narrators over Rickman’s narration. His performance improves as one goes along. When a man relates his story, it is he that speaks, and it is essential that there is a male narrating these sections. What is spoken is spoken with feeling, but not overdramatized. I have given the narration performance five stars. You do not think about the narration; it just blends in with what is said, and it works.<br /><br />This book is my favorite by the author. It was originally published in 1985. This is the first English translation. Richard Pevear and Larissa Volokhonsky are the translators. These “stories” are based on <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7728207.Svetlana_Alexievich" title="Svetlana Alexievich" rel="nofollow noopener">Svetlana Alexievich</a>’s interviews with those Soviet children who survived the Great Patriotic War. She began gathering them in the late 1970s.<br /><br />************************<br /><br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/43157098.Last_Witnesses_Unchildlike_Stories" title="Last Witnesses Unchildlike Stories by Svetlana Alexievich" rel="nofollow noopener">Last Witnesses: Unchildlike Stories</a> 5 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4025275.War_s_Unwomanly_Face" title="War's Unwomanly Face by Svetlana Alexievich" rel="nofollow noopener">War's Unwomanly Face</a> 5 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30200112.Secondhand_Time_The_Last_of_the_Soviets" title="Secondhand Time The Last of the Soviets by Svetlana Alexievich" rel="nofollow noopener">Secondhand Time: The Last of the Soviets</a> 3 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/357486.Voices_from_Chernobyl_The_Oral_History_of_a_Nuclear_Disaster" title="Voices from Chernobyl The Oral History of a Nuclear Disaster by Svetlana Alexievich" rel="nofollow noopener">Voices from Chernobyl: The Oral History of a Nuclear Disaster</a> TBR</span>
  <a data-text-id="12124158979520631832" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3567540907" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3567540907" />
    <a title="Skip" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1389748-skip"><img alt="Skip" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1231161516p2/1389748.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3567540907?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Skip" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Skip" href="/user/show/1389748-skip">Skip</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780399588754" href="/book/show/42359083-last-witnesses">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1389748-skip?shelf=award-winners">award-winners</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1389748-skip?shelf=biography-memoirs">biography-memoirs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1389748-skip?shelf=foreign-translated">foreign-translated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3567540907" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6979305598200203304">Svetlana Alexievich won a Pulitizer Prize in Literature for this book, which is a first-hand account of the experiences of about one hundred different Russian children during WWII, ranging in age from ages 2-14. The vignettes average three pages. It's horrifying and the stories while different have common themes: bombings, starvation, trying to stay warm and move to safety, fathers heading off to war, mothers protecting as best they can or dying with the children being orphaned or passed along t</span>
  <span id="freeText6979305598200203304" style="display:none">Svetlana Alexievich won a Pulitizer Prize in Literature for this book, which is a first-hand account of the experiences of about one hundred different Russian children during WWII, ranging in age from ages 2-14. The vignettes average three pages. It's horrifying and the stories while different have common themes: bombings, starvation, trying to stay warm and move to safety, fathers heading off to war, mothers protecting as best they can or dying with the children being orphaned or passed along to relatives, etc. There are some happy stories, but the book does not shy away from the psychological scars of war.<br /><br />Personally, I found it too repetitive and the short format keeps readers from getting to know characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6979305598200203304" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1412582606?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="cypt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="cypt" href="/user/show/12421486-cypt">cypt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9785949570401" href="/book/show/10380701-poslednie-svideteli">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12421486-cypt?shelf=trauminiai-pasakojimai">trauminiai-pasakojimai</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1412582606" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17329626314489492924">Read this as part of the Nobel prize hype & therefore have mixed feelings about it.<br/><br/>For one, it is a book that has you shaken. It's comprised of a 100 or so stories told by people who were children 2 to 15 years old during the WWII in Belarus. Although they're short, I couldn't read more than 10 stories in one sitting.<br/><br/>To say they are horrifying is an understatement; more like, they make you numb. Their similarity is striking - nearly all of these children have witnessed their family and neighbour</span>
  <span id="freeText17329626314489492924" style="display:none">Read this as part of the Nobel prize hype &amp; therefore have mixed feelings about it.<br /><br />For one, it is a book that has you shaken. It's comprised of a 100 or so stories told by people who were children 2 to 15 years old during the WWII in Belarus. Although they're short, I couldn't read more than 10 stories in one sitting.<br /><br />To say they are horrifying is an understatement; more like, they make you numb. Their similarity is striking - nearly all of these children have witnessed their family and neighbours murdered, helped bury them, been evacuated, lived in the care (or, "care") institutions, experienced hunger, been reunited with someone of their kin, if they were lucky. The sheer brutality of these testimonies is striking, but its repetition from story to story - even more so.<br /><br />There is no sign of Aleksejevič helself anywhere. I assume she did the talking, collecting and editing of these stories. That they are not given in any narrative order (such as, from the start of the war to salvation) allows the reader to experience the children's war as it must have been - seemingly unending, repetitious and very brutal. You won't forget it soon.<br /><br />My mixed feelings arose from the fact that this book, and some other ones, were written quite some time ago. They've been translated into my native language (Lithuanian) still during the Soviet period. Of course, the evil Nazi Germans vs the "our dear" Soviet soldiers was a convenient story to tell.<br /><br />But why the prize now? Maybe it's the committee's way to remind us that war is to be avoided at all costs, esp. with the radical right getting hold of power in Europe. Maybe it's a way to get us to be more welcome, more compassionate to the refugees to Europe. I've no idea. In any case, the prize has gone to a woman (check) who's a proponent of anti-putinist politics (check), so - very convenient indeed.<br /><br />What this book has actually taught me is to fear the pro-war propaganda even more. Right now, it's popular here in Lithuania to enlist into the army, fight the Russians if need be. Protect one's own homeland. Fucking no. If need be, I'm running away, if I only can, and taking my family. Sooner than you can finish this horrible, fantastic book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17329626314489492924" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2791771962?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maine Colonial" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maine Colonial" href="/user/show/2697302-maine-colonial">Maine Colonial</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141983554" href="/book/show/43157098-last-witnesses">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2697302-maine-colonial?shelf=world-war-ii">world-war-ii</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2697302-maine-colonial?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2697302-maine-colonial?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2791771962" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14861931554403299943">I received a free digital review copy from the publisher, via NetGalley.<br/><br/>Imagine that you are a toddler, or maybe as old as 10 or so. You and your brothers and sisters and your family and friends are living an ordinary life, except that your father is away on military service. One day, foreign soldiers rumble in on trucks and tanks and begin shooting everyone, mostly women and children. You get away, but your mother is dead, along with most people you know. Or maybe you got lucky and you, maybe w</span>
  <span id="freeText14861931554403299943" style="display:none">I received a free digital review copy from the publisher, via NetGalley.<br /><br />Imagine that you are a toddler, or maybe as old as 10 or so. You and your brothers and sisters and your family and friends are living an ordinary life, except that your father is away on military service. One day, foreign soldiers rumble in on trucks and tanks and begin shooting everyone, mostly women and children. You get away, but your mother is dead, along with most people you know. Or maybe you got lucky and you, maybe with some of your family, fled to the woods and were able to escape to a place where the soldiers didn’t attack. For long years, you work and scavenge for food, barely keeping alive, until the war ends.<br /><br />Now consider the fact that this did happen to millions of children in the USSR during World War II, and that today these children are in their late 80s and 90s. You probably know people here in the US that age. Can you imagine if the shared experience of all the people you know that age was one of childhood horror?<br /><br />If your imagination can’t quite conjure up such a nightmare, Svetlana Alexievich takes you there with this searing collection of oral histories. She conducted her interviews in the 1970s, when these children had grown into their 30s and 40s. They had so many different experiences, including joining the partisans while not yet a teenager, going into hiding, moving far from home, being taken in by relatives or acquaintances, or being put into orphanages.<br /><br />What is common to nearly all these stories is the absence of a father from the start, often the loss of a mother, and in all cases the loss of a childhood. So many of them talk about wanting to have some kind of childhood after the war ended, even if they were nearly grown up by then. Often they coveted dolls and toys, and would even buy them, just to get that feeling of being a child that had been taken from them.<br /><br />This is a difficult book to get through, but worth it. If only we had learned from WW2 and didn’t keep putting children through the trauma of war, upheaval and loss of their families.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14861931554403299943" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2899590955" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2899590955" />
    <a title="Marks54" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5109654-marks54"><img alt="Marks54" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1300926248p2/5109654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2899590955?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marks54" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marks54" href="/user/show/5109654-marks54">Marks54</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/42963288-last-witnesses">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2899590955" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8232723797984799561">I have been reading a lot about the Russo-German war in WW2 but reading Grossman’s biography and by working through Life and Fate. When I saw Last Witnesses, it was a natural addition to my list. Svetlana Alexievich is a wonderful Nobel Prize winning author who wrote The Unwomanly Face of War about the role of women in the Russian war against the Nazis. Like that book, Last Witnesses is an oral history of the experiences of Russian children following the outbreak of war in June 1941. What she ha</span>
  <span id="freeText8232723797984799561" style="display:none">I have been reading a lot about the Russo-German war in WW2 but reading Grossman’s biography and by working through Life and Fate. When I saw Last Witnesses, it was a natural addition to my list. Svetlana Alexievich is a wonderful Nobel Prize winning author who wrote The Unwomanly Face of War about the role of women in the Russian war against the Nazis. Like that book, Last Witnesses is an oral history of the experiences of Russian children following the outbreak of war in June 1941. What she has done is to assemble a large number of accounts from individuals who were asked to look back on their specific experiences at the start of the war. I suspect that many if not most of these accounts were collected when the respondents were adults - that is in the 1960s to 1980s. It is likely that this work was not published earlier because the USSR had not yet fallen. Being published now implies that most of the respondents are either no longer alive or else are over 90 years old.<br /><br />The punchline is that this is original information from direct witnesses of the German attack and all of the havoc it wreaked on the USSR. There is nobody else left to tell the story about how children experienced this mass tragedy and these witnesses are well into the process of dying off. These witnesses have a story that needs telling and a lot of material to communicate. I do not want to get into the details, but just imagine little kids talking about the start of the Holocaust from the vantage point of how it affected their lives and with a focus on specific memories.<br /><br />I understand the criticism - this is not really kid memories but the results of discussions with adults looking backwards with the help of the significant public narrative about the war and lifetimes of various efforts at remembering. Won’t these accounts be biased? Perhaps, but so what? Readers should look at the accounts and try to find the fatal biases. I did not see much. Siblings and parents are murdered. Entire villages are murdered and put to the torch. Atrocities are committed on a wide scale basis. These are not matters of conjecture or arguments. We know what happened. What is fascinating is the variation in reactions that come out in the accounts. The accounts sound reasonable and yet sound unreasonable, in that I have trouble thinking of how I would have processed these event as a kid or how my children would have seen them.<br /><br />The accounts are moving, well written, and persuasive. Each account is fairly short but consistently, the book is hard to put down. I highly recommend the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8232723797984799561" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2678632526693737358">On August 23, 1939–shortly before World War II (1939-45) broke out in Europe–enemies Nazi Germany and the Soviet Union surprised the world by signing the German-Soviet Nonaggression Pact, in which the two countries agreed to take no military action against each other for the next 10 years. <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.history.com/topics/world-war-ii/german-soviet-nonaggression-pact'>https://www.history.com/topics/world-...</a>. <br/><br/>In June of 1941, Germany began a quick rout through Mother Russia, both in the air and on the ground, killing everything in their path. This book by Svetlana Alexievic</span>
  <span id="freeText2678632526693737358" style="display:none">On August 23, 1939–shortly before World War II (1939-45) broke out in Europe–enemies Nazi Germany and the Soviet Union surprised the world by signing the German-Soviet Nonaggression Pact, in which the two countries agreed to take no military action against each other for the next 10 years. <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.history.com/topics/world-war-ii/german-soviet-nonaggression-pact">https://www.history.com/topics/world-...</a>. <br /><br />In June of 1941, Germany began a quick rout through Mother Russia, both in the air and on the ground, killing everything in their path. This book by Svetlana Alexievich is a collection of the thoughts and memories of Russian children, children of various ages from several areas of Russia, of what they remembered of the German invasion of their homeland. I am so glad that Ms. Alexievich had the foresight to collect these memories. Originally published in 1985, this English translation is courtesy of RandomHouseBooks.com, Penguin Classics. Many of these stories would have been lost without this written work, and I am grateful for this translation, courtesy of Richard Pevear and Larissa Volokhonsky. Every parent should read this book. And every soldier. <br /><br />We in the USA tend to think of war as soldiers battling soldiers, even with the obvious nightly news that proves that wrong. And we also consider it to be 'Over there'. This book is a reminder that war is Hell. Anywhere. Everywhere.<br /><br />I received a free electronic copy of this study from Netgalley, Svetlana Alexievich, and current publisher Penguin Classics. Thank you all for sharing your hard work with me. I have read this work of my own volition, and this review reflects my honest opinion of this work. <br />pub date July 2, 2019<br />Penguin Classics Random House Books<br />Reviewed July 7, 2019, at Goodreads, Netgalley, Amazon, Barnes &amp; Noble, and Kobo. Not available at BookBub. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8813991882884696590">This is a collection of memories by Soviets who were children during WWII. As you might guess, it's incredibly sad--absolutely heartbreaking. I listened to part of the audiobook and the narrations were very well done. There was so much evil and so much sorrow. But there were also incredible acts of kindness and generosity.<br/><br/>I only listened to about half of it. I do most of my audiobook listening while driving or cleaning, and I didn't want my four-year-0ld to listen with me. I plan to finish it at</span>
  <span id="freeText8813991882884696590" style="display:none">This is a collection of memories by Soviets who were children during WWII. As you might guess, it's incredibly sad--absolutely heartbreaking. I listened to part of the audiobook and the narrations were very well done. There was so much evil and so much sorrow. But there were also incredible acts of kindness and generosity.<br /><br />I only listened to about half of it. I do most of my audiobook listening while driving or cleaning, and I didn't want my four-year-0ld to listen with me. I plan to finish it at a later time. It's an easy book to put aside for a while because most of the accounts are short and aren't directly connected to the account before them. <br /><br />For several accounts I certainly wished for more closure. At the beginning of each memory, readers are told the name of the person and their age when the war began. These are accounts from survivors, but readers aren't always told how the person survived the war. One account ended with a boy in Nazi hands for helping hide weapons for Partisans--but doesn't say how he avoided execution.<br /><br />Definitely not a happy book, but an important book.</span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/42963288-last-witnesses">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73029907-cav?shelf=ww2">ww2</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73029907-cav?shelf=communism">communism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73029907-cav?shelf=russia">russia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73029907-cav?shelf=real-life-saga">real-life-saga</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73029907-cav?shelf=culture">culture</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73029907-cav?shelf=history">history</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3530234454" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11824475858412733641">I am conflicted about how to rate this one... <i>Last Witnesses</i> is a compilation of dozens of first-hand accounts from children across Europe and Russia at the onset of, and during The Second World War.<br/>Each story is fairly short; most covering only a few pages.<br/>The accounts in the book are absolutely horrific; they are actually worse than can be imagined. The stories of wholesale human misery recounted here are unimaginably terrible... <br/>Here's one that exemplifies this horror:<br/><blockquote>
  <i>"...We lived outside </i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText11824475858412733641" style="display:none">I am conflicted about how to rate this one... <i>Last Witnesses</i> is a compilation of dozens of first-hand accounts from children across Europe and Russia at the onset of, and during The Second World War.<br />Each story is fairly short; most covering only a few pages.<br />The accounts in the book are absolutely horrific; they are actually worse than can be imagined. The stories of wholesale human misery recounted here are unimaginably terrible... <br />Here's one that exemplifies this horror:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>"...We lived outside the village in German bunkers, because our village had all burned down, our old cottage had burned down and the beams for the new house. <br />Nothing of ours was left. <br />We found some army helmets in the forest and cooked in them. German helmets were big as cauldrons. We found food in the forest. It was scary going for berries and mushrooms. There were lots of German shepherds left; they attacked people and killed little children. They were used to human flesh and human blood. To its fresh scent…<br />When we went to the forest, we gathered in a big group. Some twenty of us…Our mothers taught us that we should shout as we walked in the forest, so that the dogs would get scared. While you were picking a basket of berries, you’d shout so much that you’d lose your voice. Get hoarse. Your throat would be all swollen. The dogs were big as wolves.<br />They were drawn by the human scent…"</i>
</blockquote><br />While the stories the book covers make for an important historical reference, this formatting does not make for a favorable reading experience, IMO.<br />So while I do appreciate the historical value of this book, the unusual formatting didn't really resonate with me...<br />3 stars.</span>

<div id="review_2926126854" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2926126854" />
    <a title="Keen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/66635467-keen"><img alt="Keen" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2926126854?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Keen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Keen" href="/user/show/66635467-keen">Keen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141983554" href="/book/show/43157098-last-witnesses">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2926126854" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17297658321286044430"><br/>3.5 Stars!<br/><br/>“They counted off fourteen of those who stood nearest. Gave them shovels and ordered them to dig a hole. And we were driven closer, to watch them dig…They shot three people at a time. They stood at the edge of the hole and fired point-blank. The rest of us watched. I don’t remember parents saying farewell to their children or children to their parents…They shot fourteen people and began to fill up the hole. Again we stood and watched them cover the hole with earth, trample it with boot</span>
  <span id="freeText17297658321286044430" style="display:none"><br />3.5 Stars!<br /><br />“They counted off fourteen of those who stood nearest. Gave them shovels and ordered them to dig a hole. And we were driven closer, to watch them dig…They shot three people at a time. They stood at the edge of the hole and fired point-blank. The rest of us watched. I don’t remember parents saying farewell to their children or children to their parents…They shot fourteen people and began to fill up the hole. Again we stood and watched them cover the hole with earth, trample it with boots. And they patted it down with the shovels, so it looked pretty. Neat.” <br /><br />Originally published in Russian back in 1985, this is the fifth book of Alexievich’s to be translated into English. To say that this is confronting, scarcely begins to cover it, but it does give you some idea of what lies buried within these gristly pages. This is filled with so many remarkable and countless horrific recollections and harrowing fragments which all amount to a real catalogue of terror.<br /><br />World War II took a far greater toll on innocent civilians than it did on the soldiers fighting it, and no other nation sacrificed as much as the Soviet Union did, so in many ways it seems astonishing that any children in the western USSR made it through the war alive, let alone so many.<br /><br />There is so much senseless slaughter in here. Habitual murder and killing on an industrial scale. It is disturbing to see just how inventive and barbaric so many of the Germans became in their cruelty towards women and children. It makes you wonder about the sort of circumstances that could breed such people, a culture that could result in so many freely indulging in such needless acts of widespread evil. There are countless, barbaric scenes where they seem to revel and savour the torturing of the most weak and vulnerable people.<br /><br />A problem with this is that there are just so many stories, and most are only two maybe three pages long, so you are just getting to grips with one when another one arrives. It can get a bit overwhelming, but still it gives us an idea of the sheer scale. But this shouldn’t and doesn’t detract from what Alexievich has done by bringing all of these horrendous memories together. <br /><br />It is hard to over-estimate Alexievich’s hugely vital contribution to Russian/Soviet history. Her combined body of work covers so many of the major events and tragedies which have befell the Soviet people whether it be Chernobyl, the Afghan War, The Great Patriot War or the fall of Communism. If you haven’t read her work before, you should first go to “Chernobyl Prayer” or “Zinky Boys” which show how powerful and significant the scope of her work is. All the same this was a troubling yet hugely important read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17297658321286044430" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1835102206" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1835102206" />
    <a title="Ylva" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6352413-ylva"><img alt="Ylva" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347021136p2/6352413.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1835102206?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ylva" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ylva" href="/user/show/6352413-ylva">Ylva</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780399588754" href="/book/show/42359083-last-witnesses">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6352413-ylva?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1835102206" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6138667687642616772">At first I read this book wrong - I read it like a history book. And it is, but this is oral history. There are few hard facts about WWII in the Soviet Union. There's no map at the beginning, no overview of the campaigns, no timelines or information. And this left me a bit confused. It still did at the end and I docked a star. A map at the very least would have been helpful, especially since many of the snippets mention place names and I had to look them up to see if we were in Poland or Belarus</span>
  <span id="freeText6138667687642616772" style="display:none">At first I read this book wrong - I read it like a history book. And it is, but this is oral history. There are few hard facts about WWII in the Soviet Union. There's no map at the beginning, no overview of the campaigns, no timelines or information. And this left me a bit confused. It still did at the end and I docked a star. A map at the very least would have been helpful, especially since many of the snippets mention place names and I had to look them up to see if we were in Poland or Belarus or Russia. <br /><br />This is however a very good book if you want to know what WWII was like for civilians. These are all small recollections from people who were children at the time, and while a few were old enough to join the partisans most lived out the war as civilians. <br /><br />It's the kind of book that gives you a little insight into the everyday, casual horror of war. The kind of stories where soldiers will shoot your mother in front of you and set the house on fire with the children inside, but take the time to save a handful of kittens. The various stories span a wide specter - from those who survived the ghettos of Warsaw, to those who joined the front at age 14, to those who were evacuated early and lived out the war in relative, starving peace. <br /><br />The happy endings are far between and few; by the end I had to take a few breaks. It's a good book, but it grinds the soul out of you at the same time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6138667687642616772" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1933211658" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer17910814333617962486">I'm not ready to write a proper review, but i will do this soon. <br/>UPDATE:<br/>It took me a couple of weeks to get the courage to write this review. Because, after I have finished reading it, I couldn't deal with what I have learned. I'm not even sure I can now, but I'll try.<br/>My history teacher had this saying, that history repeats itself because we never learn from the past. I believe that we want to forget.<br/>This wasn't your regular book about war. It didn't have a happy ending. Things didn't get better</span>
  <span id="freeText17910814333617962486" style="display:none">I'm not ready to write a proper review, but i will do this soon. <br />UPDATE:<br />It took me a couple of weeks to get the courage to write this review. Because, after I have finished reading it, I couldn't deal with what I have learned. I'm not even sure I can now, but I'll try.<br />My history teacher had this saying, that history repeats itself because we never learn from the past. I believe that we want to forget.<br />This wasn't your regular book about war. It didn't have a happy ending. Things didn't get better after the war was over. Families didn't reunite in the end. This was about the war. How it really was, told by people who were there. And, the shock to be even greater, it was from the point of view of those who were kids back in those days.<br />This isn't the first book that I read by Svetlana Alexievich. It's not a novel. It is composed of the memories of those who were from 2 years old to 14 years old.<br />I didn't want to read this. But I needed to know. I started with the first page and couldn't stop, even though the descriptions were so colourfull that I knew they will hunt me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17910814333617962486" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2991784300" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2991784300" />
    <a title="Claire" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10003027-claire"><img alt="Claire" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1338870028p2/10003027.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2991784300?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claire" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claire" href="/user/show/10003027-claire">Claire</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/42963288-last-witnesses">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10003027-claire?shelf=biography-memoir">biography-memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10003027-claire?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10003027-claire?shelf=social-history">social-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10003027-claire?shelf=essays">essays</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2991784300" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8815203138672619781">Never having lived in a war zone, I was hardly prepared for these vignettes. I had to pause often as I read this collection of memories of those who were children during WWII. Amazingly, the style of each is poetic, so that I wondered to what extent Alexievich had edited them. (Of course what I read was a translation, but I am assuming the translator retained the style of the original.) That I even asked that question may have been a way to gain distance.<br/><br/>Alexievich, the bio says, has spent most </span>
  <span id="freeText8815203138672619781" style="display:none">Never having lived in a war zone, I was hardly prepared for these vignettes. I had to pause often as I read this collection of memories of those who were children during WWII. Amazingly, the style of each is poetic, so that I wondered to what extent Alexievich had edited them. (Of course what I read was a translation, but I am assuming the translator retained the style of the original.) That I even asked that question may have been a way to gain distance.<br /><br />Alexievich, the bio says, has spent most of her life in Belarus, and most of the vignettes referenced Minsk, though one was of the seige of Leningrad. The age range of the children at the start of the war ranges from 0-13--one had not been born at the beginning of the war and one 2 year old memory was told.<br /><br />I did not see a pattern to the arrangement other than alternating between the very young and 10-13 year olds. Although all were deeply moving, some were more horrible than others--they were spread out, and the last several seemed to have more detail about the victory.<br /><br />What amazed me most was children having to see their parents shot, then having to make decisions about what to do. Others had been left at home and had to decide between waiting for mother to return or evacuate when other neighbors were leaving. Some had an older sibling, but others (6-8 year olds) were the older sibling. Some hunted Mama. Some started out with a parent, but got separated. <br /><br />Hunger was ever present: the siege of Leningrad, 900 days; hiding in the forests; orphanages making do with what they had.<br /><br />Many of the 12-13 year olds wanted to help fight; some did though the official age for joining was 16. One told of shooting a man.<br /><br />All telling events no child should have to experience.<br /><br />And all the while, as I read, I couldn't help think that there are children having these experiences now, caused not by Germans but by us, directly in Iraq and Afghanistan, indirectly in Yemen and Syria. This is a book all leaders should read before the choice is made to go to war. This is a book people like me who have not experienced war first hand need to read. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8815203138672619781" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('66tbNgfywUvusEdCFMnDazJVofNUJjDS0Eyjs/ti5Y4t1pQVO/0mIjCMcnVOpFYZUpq3HVpCTX/hwjJc+5Muog==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

It is 1971, a year after the Biafran War, and Nigeria is under military rule—though the politics of the state matter less than those of her home to Enitan Taiwo, an eleven-year-old girl tired of waiting for school to start. Will her mother, who has become deeply religious since the death of Taiwo\'s brother, allow her friendship with the new girl next door, the brash and beautiful Sheri Bakare? Everything Good Will Come charts the fate of these two African girls, one born of privilege and the other, a lower class &quot;half-caste&quot;; one who is prepared to manipulate the traditional system while the other attempts to defy it.\n\nWritten in the voice of Enitan, the novel traces this unusual friendship into their adult lives, against the backdrop of tragedy, family strife, and a war-torn Nigeria. In the end, Everything Good Will Come is Enitan\'s story; one of a fiercely intelligent, strong young woman coming of age in a culture that still insists on feminine submission. Enitan bucks the familial and political systems until she is confronted with the one desire too precious to forfeit in the name of personal freedom: her desire for a child. Everything Good Will Come evokes the sights and smells of Africa while imparting a wise and universal story of love, friendship, prejudice, survival, politics, and the cost of divided Iš viso Grigorijus Kanovičius parašė 11 romanų, kurie sudaro savotišką Lietuvos žydų gyvenimo sagą, apimančią laikotarpį nuo XVIII amžiaus iki mūsų dienų.\nNaujausias kūrinys „Miestelio romansas“ – autentiškų prisiminimų romanas, kuriame pasakojama, kaip 1920–1941 metų istoriniai įvykiai keitė Lietuvos miestelių gyventojų – žydų, lietuvių, lenkų ir rusų – likimus. „Ši knyga – tarsi tų ikikarinių dienų atspindys, lyg paviršiun iškeltas lietuviškas „Titanikas“, iš nebūties sąnašų drauge su savo spalvingais keleiviais prikeliantis anų dienų problemas, kurios aktualios ir šiandien“, – pristato Kanovičius.\n\nPasakojimo centre – autoriaus šeima, kieto būdo senelė Rocha, tėvai – amatininkų bei meistrų palikuonys, jų pamatinės vertybės – šeima, vaikai, sąžiningas darbas, profesinė garbė, ištikimybė tėvų ir protėvių kapams, jų atminimui, gyvenimas pagal Biblijos priesakus. O greta – ir Jonavos lietuviai, su kuriais per amžius žydai gyveno taikiai ir santarvėje, – spalvinga miestelio žmonių galerija.\n\nUnikalus rašytojo stilius – metaforų gausa, biblinės paralelės, liaudies filosofija, humoro ir lyrizmo derinys. It will be years before he understands that his burgeoning friendship with the Vietnam vet Rodney Falk, a strange and solitary man, will reshape his life, or that he will become obsessed with Rodney\'s mysterious past.\n\nWhy does Rodney shun the world? Why does he accept and befriend the narrator? And what really happened at the mysterious ‘My Khe\' incident? Many years pass with these questions unanswered; the two friends drift apart. But as the narrator\'s literary career takes off, his personal life collapses. Suddenly, impossibly, the novelist finds that Rodney\'s fate and his own are linked, and the story spirals towards its fascinating, surreal conclusion.       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_49014434'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/49014434-mes-vilko-vaikai-palikti-ryt-pr-sijoje?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Mes – vilko vaikai. Palikti Rytų Prūsijoje.<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/7495707.Sonya_Winterberg?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Sonya Winterberg<\/a><span title=\"Goodreads Author!\">*<\/span>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.81 avg rating &mdash; 37 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2012\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer17765686776994373215\">Knygoje „Mes – vilko vaikai“ vokiškai rašanti švedų-suomių žurnalistė Sonya Winterberg pasakoja apie 1944-uosius metus, kai Raudonoji Armija kirto Rytų Prūsijos sieną, priversdama milijonus vietinių vokiečių trauktis į vakarus.\n\nTais metais tūkstanči<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17765686776994373215\" style=\"display:none\">Knygoje „Mes – vilko vaikai“ vokiškai rašanti švedų-suomių žurnalistė Sonya Winterberg pasakoja apie 1944-uosius metus, kai Raudonoji Armija kirto Rytų Prūsijos sieną, priversdama milijonus vietinių vokiečių trauktis į vakarus.\n\nTais metais tūkstančiai Karaliaučiaus regiono be tėvų likusių vaikų tapo šios katastrofos įkaitais ir Lietuva, ką tik vėl okupuota Sovietį Sąjungos, jiems atrodė paskutine išsigelbėjimo viltimi. Ten buvo maisto, todėl vaikų grupės, nuo visai mažylių iki paauglių leidosi į sunkią kelionę. Jie važiavo ant traukinių stogų, kėlėsi plaustais per Nemuną. Tūkstančiai benamių, alkanų vaikų apsigyveno Lietuvos miškuose, prašinėdami maisto arba vagiliaudami. Kai kuriems pasisekdavo rasti darbo lietuvių ūkiuose, bet šeimininkai dažniausiai rinkdavosi stipriausius, tai išskirdami brolius ir seseris. Visa tai vykdavo nelegaliai, ir ne vienas valstietis už geraširdiškumą sumokėdavo tremtimi į Sibirą.\n\nSonya Winterberg knygoje „Mes – vilko vaikai“ pateikia daugybę realių istorijų ir statistikos. Pavyzdžiui, ji pateikia apytikrį skaičių vokiečių vaikų, pokaryje atsiradusių Lietuvoje. Sonya Winterberg rašo: „..1950 m. gegužę Lietuvoje buvo užregistruoti 3274 Kaliningrado srities vokiečiai. Tarp jų buvo 770 vaikų, kuriuos lydėjo bent vienas iš tėvų, ir 365 našlaičiai. Bet tikrieji skaičiai yra gerokai didesni...“. Kiekviena „Mes – vilko vaikai“ istorija yra nepaprastai skaudi.\n\nPavyzdžiui, štai vienas iš Sonya Winterberg užfiksuotų pasakojimų. Ursula Hundrieser gimė 1933 metais Karaliaučiuje. Jos tėvas ir vyresni broliai žuvo kare. Priartėjus sovietų armijai, Ursulai, jos motinai ir mažesniems broliams pasitraukti į Vakarus nepavyko. Raudonarmiečiai išprievartavo Ursulos mamą vaikų akyse. Po patirtos traumos Ursulos mama miršta ligoninėje. Po to iš bado miršta jos vienerių metų brolis. Dvylikametė mergaitė bėga į Lietuvos miškus ir pradeda prašyti išmaldos. Galiausiai ji suranda ją priglaudusią valstiečių šeimą. Ursula dirba sunkiai, bet turi stogą ir maistą. Vieną dieną ją sužaloja pasibaidęs arklys. Ligoninėje mergaitę priima kaip vokietę, ir sovietų gydytojas atsisako gydyti „fašistų vaiką“ ir net uždraudžia seselėms sustabdyti kraujavimą. Represijų išsigandęs valstietis palieka Ursalą šalia ligoninės, bet kraujuojanti mergaitei vis tiek sugeba grįžti į ūkį. Šiaip ne taip apsigydžiusi Ursala prisijungia prie klajojančių &quot;vilko vaikų&quot;. Vėliau ją įdarbina kita lietuvių šeima. When the city is bombed, she seeks refuge across southern France, witnessing countless horrors: children torn from their parents, mothers throwing themselves under buses.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/19003531.Svetlana_Alexievich">About Svetlana Alexievich</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/19003531.Svetlana_Alexievich"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1599555641p3/19003531.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11192449874928798595">Svetlana Alexievich was born in Ivano Frankivsk, Ukraine. Her father was Belarusian and her mother Ukrainian. Alexievich grew up in Belarus, where both her parents were teachers. She studied to be a journalist at the University of Minsk and worked a teacher, journalist and editor. In Minsk she has worked at the newspaper Sel'skaja Gazeta, Alexievich's criticism of the political regimes in the Sovi</span>
  <span id="freeText11192449874928798595" style="display:none">Svetlana Alexievich was born in Ivano Frankivsk, Ukraine. Her father was Belarusian and her mother Ukrainian. Alexievich grew up in Belarus, where both her parents were teachers. She studied to be a journalist at the University of Minsk and worked a teacher, journalist and editor. In Minsk she has worked at the newspaper Sel'skaja Gazeta, Alexievich's criticism of the political regimes in the Soviet Union and thereafter Belarus has periodically forced her to live abroad, for example in Italy, France, Germany and Sweden.<br /><br />Svetlana Alexievich depicts life during and after the Soviet Union through the experience of individuals. In her books she uses interviews to create a collage of a wide range of voices. With her "documentary novels", Svetlana Alexievich, who is a journalist, moves in the boundary between reporting and fiction. Her major works are her grand cycle Voices of Utopia, which consists of five parts. Svetlana Alexievich's books criticize political regimes in both the Soviet Union and later Belarus. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11192449874928798595" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

Creating controversy and outrage when it was first published in the USSR—it was called by reviewers there a “slanderous piece of fantasy” and part of a “hysterical chorus of malign attacks”—Zinky Boys presents the candid and affecting testimony of the officers and grunts, nurses and prostitutes, mothers, sons, and daughters who describe the war and its lasting effects. What emerges is a story that is shocking in its brutality and revelatory in its similarities to the American experience in Vietnam. The Soviet dead were shipped back in sealed zinc coffins (hence the term “Zinky Boys”), while the state denied the very existence of the conflict. Svetlana Alexievich brings us the truth of the Soviet-Afghan War: the beauty of the country and the savage Army bullying, the killing and the mutilation, the profusion of Western goods, the shame and shattered lives of returned veterans. Bringing together dozens <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText4889415173871433381\" style=\"display:none\">From the 2015 winner of the Nobel Prize in Literature, Svetlana Alexievich, comes the first English translation of her latest work, an oral history of the disintegration of the Soviet Union and the emergence of a new Russia. Bringing together dozens of voices in her distinctive documentary style, Secondhand Time is a monument to the collapse of the USSR, charting the decline of Soviet culture and speculating on what will rise from the ashes of Communism. As in all her books, Alexievich gives voice to women and men whose stories are lost in the official narratives of nation-states, creating a powerful alternative history from the personal and private stories of individuals. Svetlana Alexievich was born in the Ukraine in 1948 and grew up in Belarus. As a newspaper journalist, she spent her early career in Minsk compiling first-hand accounts of World War II, the Soviet-Afghan War, the fall of the Berlin Wall and the Chernobyl meltdown. Her unflinching work—‘the whole of our history…is a huge common grave and a bloodbath’—earned her persecution from the Lukashenko regime and she was forced to emigrate. She lived in Paris, Gothenburg and Berlin before returning to Minsk in 2011. She has won a number of prizes, including the National Book Critics Circle Award, the Prix Médicis, and the Oxfam Novib/PEN Award. In 2015, she was awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature. Bela Shayevich is a writer, translator and illustrator. Her translations have appeared in journals such as Little Star, St. Petersburg Review, and Calque. She was the editor of n+1 magazine’s translations of the Pussy Riot closing statements. Of Alexievich’s writing, she says it is ‘resounding with nothing but the truth’.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"4889415173871433381\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return
